News in Depth: Slow-Motion U.S. Recovery Searches for Second Gear --- Economists See 2014 As Best Year for Growth Amid Improvements In Home Sales and Jobs; Some Firms Still Skittish
By Brenda Cronin
1535 words
25 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
14
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

After four bumpy years, the U.S. recovery finally appears to be on a smoother road.

Many economists now predict 2014 will be the best year for growth since 2005, while joblessness is expected to click below 7% next year for the first time since 2008. Houses are selling again, the energy sector is booming and jobs, while not plentiful, are being created at a steady pace. On Wednesday, the Federal Reserve upped its estimate for next year's expansion as well.

There have been earlier waves of optimism that the economy was poised for better growth, and the past week's financial-market turmoil is a reminder that a lot still could go wrong.

News that the Fed could start dialing back stimulus later this year sent the Dow Jones Industrial Average down 2.34% on Thursday. The average closed at 14799.40 Friday, down 1.80% for the week. A persistent decline in stock prices or faster-than-expected increases in mortgage and other long-term interest rates could slow the recovery.

It has been a long haul. The recovery that began in June 2009 has been painfully slow. Jobs, median household income, industrial production and home prices still haven't returned to the levels they were at before the recession.

But despite the weak pace of overall growth, the recovery has proved surprisingly resilient. The economy has absorbed a series of shocks, from tax hikes to a tsunami in Japan, without getting knocked off course or falling back into recession. And crucially, inflation remains low.

"The good news is if [the recovery] continues to ratchet up gradually," the economy could see growth persist and strengthen, says Nariman Behravesh, chief economist at IHS Global Insight.

It will take a prolonged upturn to make up ground lost during the recession, which stretched from December 2007 through June 2009. Long-term unemployment, a hallmark of the recession's toll, remains at historically high levels. The economy has been expanding, but at a 2.2% annual pace, below the average 3.3% rate of recent decades. The federal government's belt-tightening has weighed on the recovery, but that impact is now dissipating.

Economists in the latest Wall Street Journal monthly survey expect gross domestic product to expand at a 2.3% annual pace this year and 2.8% next year. The Federal Reserve edged up 2014 growth forecasts to between 3% and 3.5%, from a March estimate of 2.9% to 3.4%.

After the central bank's policy-making committee met last week, Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke explained the panel's recent optimism by saying the economy's "fundamentals look a little better to us."

Such optimism about the recovery looks justified, thanks in part to the energy boom and the real-estate sector's patchy comeback. The property market's expected contribution to the economy during the second half of this year is the critical engine, says Joseph LaVorgna, chief U.S. economist at Deutsche Bank, noting that housing gains benefit many other areas, from furniture sales to landscaping.

Earlier in the recovery, similar bouts of optimism flared, only to be snuffed out by events such as the tsunami in Japan, euro-zone turmoil and budget strife in Washington. While such shocks undercut the momentum, they didn't tip the economy back into recession. But each disappointment renewed skittishness among consumers and businesses scarred by the financial crisis.

"We're extremely cautious and not seeing dramatic growth," says Mike DePasquale, chief executive of BIO-key International, Inc. The Wall, N.J., company makes security software that recognizes biometric markers, such as fingerprints. In recent months, BIO-key added three employees, bringing its full-time staff to 19.

Although demand at home and abroad remains brisk, the ordeal of the recession, when BIO-key laid off workers and streamlined operations, is still vivid. "We all sense that things are beginning to get better, but we don't innately feel it," Mr. DePasquale says. "There are still so many people that each of us know who are out of work or struggling in positions that are below where they were five years ago."

Such doubts are inevitable amid a recovery that has defied the contours and pace of previous comebacks. The labor market remains strained, even as the unemployment rate has dropped to 7.6%, from double-digit levels. The U.S. has 2.4 million fewer jobs today than when the recession began. Adjusting for population growth, it will take more than nine years at the current rate of hiring to return to prerecession employment levels, according to estimates from the Brookings Institution.

Slow hiring and scant wage growth have made the recovery feel no different from the recession for some. According to the latest WSJ/NBC News poll, 58% of Americans believe the country is in a recession.

That isn't surprising after a financial crisis that put households through their "worst losses of wealth in 80 years," says Robert Shapiro, chairman of Sonecon LLC, an advisory firm.

And yet, consumer confidence now stands at a five-year high. Households that borrowed extensively before the recession have pared their debt. After years of cutting back, they are spending again, moving beyond absolute essentials to big-ticket purchases that were postponed in leaner times.

Among those consumers is Abbie Batia, who says she and her husband had to "bite the bullet" and buy a bigger car to accommodate their growing family.

The Batias, of Orlando, Fla., have three children, ages 4, 2 and 9 months. They replaced their sport-utility vehicle -- which was all paid for, Mrs. Batia says -- with a larger SUV less than a year ago. Mrs. Batia, a part-time adjunct professor of exercise and sports science, and her husband, who works at a construction-management firm, are feeling more optimistic about the economy than they did during the recession, but remain careful about spending.

"We were definitely pinching pennies more four years ago than we are now," says Mrs. Batia, who is 34. The family keeps to a monthly budget, she says, "but we haven't been hurting in other areas when we've had to add money to the grocery fund."

Economists see consumers now carrying this recovery forward, as a combination of slow gains in the job market and a snapback in housing improves their mood.

Household balance sheets have shown "pretty good improvement," but about 20% of mortgage borrowers are still underwater on their loans, says Paul Ashworth, chief U.S. economist at research firm Capital Economics. Businesses have reined in borrowing and strengthened their books, he says, but remain gun-shy about spending to expand.

"What we really want is business investment to rise well above just replacing worn-out stuff, and into getting new stuff," he says, "because when you get extra new stuff, you need new staff. When you buy two new vehicles, you need two new drivers."

Many businesses say they don't want to commit to new investment until they are sure that demand is there. Some continue to cut back in the face of wavering demand.

Aleris Corp., a Cleveland-based aluminum processor with plants in the U.S., Europe and China, is hoping that America's housing rebound translates into increased sales. The company sells aluminum for gutters and other household uses, as well as for appliances such as washers and dryers that people often buy when moving into new homes.

"We're looking forward to that big jump in demand," says Sean Stack, the company's chief financial officer.

In 2011, the company restarted its U.S. building-materials plant, which it had shut during the housing bust, but it is running at just 65% of capacity. Mr. Stack says he hopes demand will pick up this summer, based on the recent pace of home building. He adds the company has yet to see much improvement in demand in the division that provides aluminum for consumer products.

As a result, Aleris has been cutting costs, pulling back on travel spending, and trimming jobs -- about 150 white-collar positions so far this year in the U.S. and Europe.

The company hasn't applied the brakes across the board. It is moving forward with investments in facilities in North Carolina, Belgium and a new $350 million aluminum-rolling plant in Zhenjiang, China. In China, however, the company has seen "pockets of softness" after years of breakneck growth, Mr. Stack says.

Weakness overseas remains a wild card for the recovery. If the euro-zone recession gets worse and softness in China and elsewhere persists, that could stall momentum in the U.S. There also are risks closer to home. Fiscal clashes in Washington could further strain consumers and businesses. Already, the federal government has cut spending 4.5% since June 2009, compared with an average 6.3% increase in recoveries since 1970.

---

Ben Casselman contributed to this article.

Commercial Property: No, That Isn't Freedom Tower
By Sarah Rose
845 words
24 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

When Richard Blanco read his stirring poem "One Today" at President Barack Obama's second inaugural ceremony in January, he spoke of "the last floor on the Freedom Tower jutting into a sky that yields to our resilience."

That prompted Jordan Barowitz, the director of external affairs for the Durst Organization, a part owner of the tower, to tweet: "Blanco, we like the shout out. But it's One World Trade Center!"

The tallest building at the World Trade Center site is in the midst of a bit of an identity crisis as it nears completion. While the development's name was officially changed to One World Trade Center in 2009, many still refer to it as the Freedom Tower, the name it was given in 2003 when the trauma from the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attack was still fresh.

"Not for long," says Douglas Durst, whose organization became a partner in the project, with the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, in 2010. He says the old name is fading quickly and, most important, there's no confusion among prospective tenants. "We're talking to a lot of people and it doesn't come up," he says.

But Freedom Tower still sticks with many members of the public.

"It was so dramatic and poignant, as we were sailing away from America, the last two things we saw were the Statue of Liberty and the Freedom Tower," says Gracie Best, a native of New Hampshire who recently relocated to England with her husband aboard the Queen Mary 2.

In May, there was a flurry of media coverage as construction crews added a spire to the top of the building. Many of the stories used the old name.

"Tonight, the Freedom Tower becomes the tallest building in the Western hemisphere," proclaimed Diane Sawyer on ABC's "World News."

"The Freedom Tower, rising from ashes," read a CNN headline with a story from John Avlon, the senior political columnist for the Daily Beast and Newsweek. After being corrected via Twitter, Mr. Avlon replied: "I'm always going to call it the Freedom Tower."

On Sunday, he said via email that referring to it that way "is a defiant matter of pride."

Buildings are often retitled when anchor tenants move. But landmarks frustrate attempts at rebranding: The Met Life building on Park Avenue is still the Pan Am building to many, even though the airline ceased operations in 1991. Citi Field replaced Shea Stadium, which was torn down in 2009, yet many Mets fans seem not to have noticed.

"When it comes to naming buildings, it's actually very difficult to change the name," says James Fox, chief executive of Red Peak Branding. "Names stick."

When Chicago's Sears Tower became the Willis Tower in 2009, a national marketing campaign was launched. "We thought we had to make a really big public deal out of it, to get the word out," says Bob Wislow, the chairman and CEO of US Equities, which manages the 4.56-million-square-foot building.

Of course, One World Trade Center is no ordinary building. For some, it will forever be associated with the 2001 terrorist attack that killed nearly 3,000 people.

The Freedom Tower name was coined two years after the attacks by then-Gov. George Pataki as the rebuilding effort slowly moved forward.

Many felt that the name and the tower's height -- 1,776 feet -- were important symbols of the resolve and determination on the part of the American people to fight terrorism.

But some minds changed as the rebuilding process advanced. Quietly and without fanfare in 2009, the Port Authority officially changed the name to One World Trade Center. The old name was considered emotionally charged and some felt that it might hinder the Port's leasing efforts, forever associating the building with the profound loss suffered on Sept. 11.

"It's an office building and not a memorial and not a monument," says Mr. Durst, who wasn't involved in the renaming but says he supported the change.

One World Trade is slated to open to the public and tenants in early 2015. About 55% of the building's approximately 3 million square feet are leased to the media company Conde Nast, the federal government, and a Chinese developer.

Mr. Durst says prospective tenants today look at One World Trade Center as a modern building at a great location. Security is no more of a concern than at any other building, he says: "Time has gone by and people are concerned about other things."

But some refuse to give up the Freedom Tower name, including people who were involved in the site's redevelopment.

"I always say Freedom Tower," says Daniel Liebskind, the architect who led the redevelopment plan for the World Trade Center site. "The truth is that in the master plan, the notion of a 1,776-foot tower does ultimately refer to freedom."

Commercial Property -- Dynasties / A Look at New York's Real Estate Families: Gural Scion on Fast Track
By Anjali Athavaley
1061 words
24 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

As the scion of the Gural real-estate family, Jeff Gural has spent most of his career running the family's buildings and helping the growth of the Manhattan brokerage firm Newmark Grubb Knight Frank.

These days, he's shifted his attention to his race-track investments: Mr. Gural is majority owner of two tracks in upstate New York and leases another from the state of New Jersey. While he was largely motivated by his love of horse racing when he bought them, he also recognized their investment potential -- especially if casinos were one day permitted on the sites.

Now that goal may soon be realized. If Gov. Andrew Cuomo succeeds in getting approval for four new casino resorts in the state, Mr. Gural says he would hope to convert his Tioga Downs racino in Nichols, N.Y., into one of them. "I think I have the best location in the Southern Tier," says the 70-year-old chairman of Newmark Grubb Knight Frank.

Mr. Gural says he can pay more attention to his race tracks because he has a deep bench in the New York property business, made up of a younger generation of the family. His son Eric Gural, who is 45, and his nephew Brian Steinwurtzel, 36, both executives at Newmark, have taken over the family's properties in the city. The two have divided the portfolio in half, with Eric mainly focusing on buildings that were acquired in earlier years, Mr. Gural says.

Jeff Gural is still involved with financing issues, because he has relationships with the family's lenders. Otherwise, he jokes: "I just oversee and make sure they don't do anything totally stupid."

The family's portfolio includes 8.5 million square feet of real estate, mostly in the city, worth about $3 billion. The Gurals mainly own office buildings that used to be occupied by manufacturers and are now filled with smaller tenants such as nonprofits and theater companies. The properties are all owned with partners, Mr. Gural says.

The family's ties to real estate date back to Mr. Gural's granduncles, Leon and Maurice Spear, of Spear & Co., a brokerage firm, and Aaron Rabinowitz of Fred F. French, a developer. Mr. Gural's father, Aaron, graduated from college with an accounting degree during the Great Depression and took a job at Spear & Co. reading water and electric meters.

After going off on his own as a broker, in the 1950s he joined Newmark, where he and his partners started buying commercial buildings in Manhattan, such as the Film Center Building, at 630 Ninth Ave., and 515 Madison, the historic DuMont building.

It was Jeff Gural and his partner, Barry Gosin, now Newmark's chief executive, who accelerated the pace of acquisitions in the 1970s and 1980s, buying up properties such as 40 Worth St., now the New York offices of Gap Inc., and the Dean & Deluca building on Broadway in SoHo, a street that was home primarily to manufacturing tenants at the time.

"We basically changed that whole neighborhood," Mr. Gural says. "That was the first major transformation."

Their investments are now paying off, family members say. The older loft buildings are becoming increasingly popular with a wide range of tenants.

In the past decade, the family has focused more on renovating its buildings than acquiring new ones. It's currently working on a project with SL Green Realty Corp. to combine 1560 Broadway with a neighboring building to create a bigger retail space. The project, which costs between $50 million and $100 million, is expected to be completed early next year, Mr. Steinwurtzel says. The family is also in the process of doing a gut renovation on a roughly 100,000-square-foot office building at 45 W. 34th St., a $10 million to $15 million project.

As for Mr. Gural's interest in race tracks, he became interested in horse racing as a teenager. "I used to go to the race track in high school," he says. "It was actually very popular back then. That was the only form of gambling in America."

He runs the Meadowlands Racetrack, a harness racing track in East Rutherford, N.J., where he's spending $100 million to rebuild the grandstand and seating area. He says he is making improvements with the hopes that state law will one day permit a casino there. The family thinks the Meadowlands has the potential to become one of its most successful investments, if casino gambling were allowed.

In New York, the track business isn't a big money maker, Mr. Gural says. Business improved when he converted Tioga Downs and Vernon Downs, located in Vernon, N.Y., into so-called "racinos," which offer high-speed slot machines but no table gambling. But in the case of Vernon Downs, competition has been tough from a full Indian casino five miles away.

Vernon Downs "was just a mistake," Mr. Gural says. The racino doesn't make money, he says, and a recent agreement with the state that grants the Oneida Indian Nation exclusive rights to casino gaming in the region has sealed its fate.

He could have better luck with Tioga Downs, though. Last week, Mr. Cuomo and legislative leaders struck an agreement to authorize four casino resorts upstate if voters pass a constitutional amendment in November to expand non-Indian-tribe gambling. If the amendment is approved, Mr. Gural says he would bid for a casino license.

There's no guarantee that effort would be successful. His foray into the racing business upstate was always somewhat of a gamble in itself. "The only way it'll probably turn out to be a good investment is if I'm able to get a casino license at Tioga Downs," he says. "But it's fun."

Even if it doesn't happen, he says he'll still love the horses. Mr. Gural owns two breeding farms upstate and about 100 horses.

"If you took my father into a casino, he wouldn't make a bet," says Eric Gural, who grew up going to race tracks. "That's not what it's about . . . . He loves racing. He loves the sport."

Construction Employment Inches Back
By Laura Kusisto
730 words
21 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Vacant lots and cranes dangling over half-finished buildings have been among the most visible signs of the region's weak economy, but jobs numbers released Thursday suggested that picture may be improving.

The construction industry showed some of the first signs of sustained recovery in May: New York state, New Jersey and Connecticut all added significant numbers of jobs in the sector, even as overall job growth remained spotty.

Economists said the growth in construction-related employment was driven by large development projects getting off the ground, stalled sites restarting and an uptick in single-family home construction.

Connecticut's construction jobs grew 9% this May compared with last year -- much more quickly than the overall private-sector job market, which grew 0.9% over the past year.

"We're not out of the woods in the housing market, but we are getting there," said Andrew Condon, director of the office of research at the Connecticut Department of Labor.

Connecticut issued permits for 337 new housing units in April of this year, compared to 222 the same month last year, according to U.S. Census data.

Even with the upward trend, however, Connecticut has recovered less than half of the jobs lost during the downturn, while New York has gained back all the jobs it lost and more and New Jersey has gained back about 60% of the jobs it lost.

Mr. Condon said new construction jobs were good news because the overall economic recovery is "highly dependent" on the housing market.

New York City's construction jobs grew 1.9% in May from April, compared with overall private-sector job growth of 0.4%. May marked the fifth consecutive month in which construction experienced year-over-year growth in the city, after it took a hit immediately following superstorm Sandy, which struck the region Oct. 29.

Elena Volovelsky, an economist with the New York state Department of Labor, said that could signal that construction was coming back.

New York Building Congress President Richard T. Anderson said his organization was working on a report, to be released next week, that projects New York City construction employment will reach 130,000 jobs in 2014. That would be the first time construction employment surpassed 130,000 since the 2008 peak of 132,600 jobs.

Construction of residential towers, office buildings and educational and health-care facilities "has come roaring back," Mr. Anderson said. Banks are making more construction loans for residential projects and consumer demand remains strong, he said. "Office buildings that have been on the drawing board longer than anticipated are finally starting to go forward."

A number of stalled condo developments, such as 150 Charles St. in the West Village and 56 Leonard St. in TriBeCa, are moving forward. Large office projects are also starting to progress on the far West Side.

Overall job growth in the city was slow this month, according to Barbara Byrne Denham, an economist at real-estate firm Eastern Consolidated. New York added 3,100 private-sector jobs in May, when the numbers are seasonally adjusted, according to a report she released Thursday.

"After adding more than 40,000 jobs in the first four months of this year -- or more than 10,000 per month, on average -- May's small job gain is disappointing," Ms. Denham wrote.

New Jersey's construction jobs grew about 5% in May to 134,800 from 128,600 a year ago. That compared with overall private-sector job growth of about 2% in the past year.

Jay Grant a home builder and recent past president of the Community Builders and Remodelers Association, said he recently built a new home in Madison, N.J., and had six written offers within two weeks of putting it on the market.

"I've been in the business for 25 years, I can't remember the last time I had six written offers. I'm not the only builder that's having that experience," Mr. Grant said.

Mr. Condon, of the Connecticut Labor Department, said it was too early to tell whether his state's recovery is solid.

"Whether or not that is real and sustainable or a result of some of the speculation that some markets are seeing, I don't know the answer to that," he said.
